down?","dateCreated":"2020-05-01T06:05:56.000Z","datePublished":"2020-05-01T06:05:56.000Z","dateModified":"2020-05-01T09:31:40.111Z","isAccessibleForFree":true,"articleBody":"It was spruiked as a mission of mercy, airlifting urgent medical supplies from Wuhan to Australia as hospitals here prepared for the full onslaught of COVID-19. But the flight that landed at Sydney airport on the night of April 8, with some 70 tonnes of PPE (Personal Protection Equipment) in its cargo hold, was, in fact, part of a commercial venture in a coronavirus-spooked market. 7NEWS.com.au can now reveal that the Australian government never requested or accepted what was on the plane. In the video above: Wuhan plane touches down in Sydney The Suparna privately chartered plane &ndash; with its crew under strict Australian Border Force instructions not to leave the airport premises - left for its return flight to Wuhan in the early hours of the following morning. On board was 11,298 tins of Viplus baby formula and 35,000 kgs of Tasmanian Atlantic salmon. The Australian government will not say how much the Chinese consortium was asking for the unsolicited 500 cubic metres of cargo, comprising one million face masks, protective gowns, goggles, gloves, shoe covers, antiseptic wipes and coronavirus testing kits, all purportedly &ldquo;European certified&rdquo;. Whatever the figure, it was too much. The deal did not go ahead. 7NEWS.com.au is unable to say to what extent the asking price reflected the impact of worldwide shortages on the price the consortium paid to its supplier, or the mark-up made by the consortium when it attempted to sell the material in Australia. A spokesman for the Department of Health confirmed to 7NEWS.com.au it received &ldquo;a number of approaches offering products at inflated prices&rdquo; at a time when PPE supplies were in short supply. It did not deny, however, that one of those unsolicited offers involved a shipment that arrived by plane from Wuhan in April. The Sydney Airport Corporation has confirmed to 7NEWS there was only one flight that landed in Sydney in April from Wuhan. It was the one hailed as a mission of mercy by a Sydney-based Chinese entrepreneur who bears a multitude of office-bearing titles in Australia and is admired as a Chinese Communist Party stalwart in his home province of Hunan in South Central China. When 7NEWS approached Yuan &ldquo;Richard&rdquo; Zuwen on Wednesday about the fate of the cargo that arrived on a privately chartered Suparna aircraft in Sydney on April 8, he declined to give details. &ldquo;Quite a number of local and Chinese business communities have been involved in the 8 April shipment, I am not in the position to comment at this stage,&rdquo; he said in a statement. 7NEWS has also been unsuccessful in ascertaining where all that baby formula ended up, with Yuan saying it was going to be &ldquo;donated to the Hubei Youth Development Foundation which will distribute the goods to key hospitals, schools, children&rsquo;s health centres, kindergartens and nursing homes&rdquo;. Baby formula supplies have been threatened in Australia in recent months, and scenes of long queues, panic buying and even verbal and physical abuse captured outside stores seeking to ration purchases of the products. And scenes of long queues, panic buying and even verbal and physical abuse have been captured outside stores seeking to ration purchases of the products. The shortage has been blamed in some quarters on Daigou traders &ndash; people living in Australia and turning a profit by buying up products in high demand and then selling them to wealthy consumers living in mainland China. Yuan is the Executive Director of the Australia China Daigou Association. Less than a month before that plane landed, Yuan founded an organisation called the Australia China Goodwill Association. On April 9, as chair of that association, he issued a media release hailing Suparna flight Y87447 as a mission of mercy and a victory in the China-Australia bilateral relationship in a time of crisis. &ldquo;We&rsquo;re motivated by the classic Aussie response of helping a mate in need,&rdquo; he wrote. &ldquo;Our community has been honoured by the opportunity to arrange this bilateral goodwill measure &ndash; coordinated to support people in need both in Australia and China.&rdquo; Diplomatic claims Yuan said in the release the shipment was coordinated through &ldquo;Australian diplomatic&rdquo; channels. A spokesman for Prime Minister Scott Morrison has told 7NEWS Yuan was given no such diplomatic access. But Yuan appears fond of spruiking his powerful connections, having posted online an extensive collection of photos featuring the Chinese businessman meeting Australian politicians online. In a series of photos, Yuan, whose business interests include an immigration and visa agency for Chinese nationals, says he &ldquo;participated in the small reception of the Australian Prime Minister and his &lsquo;top team&rsquo; and had a chat with the PM &hellip; on &lsquo;improving Sino-Australian relations&rsquo; and other aspects&rdquo;. Yuan has also been snapped on occasion with Immigration Minister David Coleman, Trade Minister Simon Birmingham, former NSW Premier Bob Carr and even former prime minister John Howard. Here he is with the now Home Affairs Minister Peter Dutton: And here he is with former Foreign Affairs Minister Julie Bishop: Yuan&rsquo;s political connections have attracted considerable media attention in his home country. That includes a full-length profile in the Chinese Communist Party-controlled publication Chinanews.com explaining how Yuan &rdquo;helped&rdquo; the Australian PM in his handling of the coronavirus. &ldquo;Yuan Zuwen has had a relationship with Scott Morrison for many years, and he (Yuan) felt it necessary to pass him some information and ideas,&rdquo; the story, translated into English reads. Yuan wrote a letter urging the PM to be more proactive and flexible in responding to the pandemic and &ldquo;keep in mind the friendly relations between Australia and China more comprehensively.&rdquo; The story captured with enthusiasm Yuan&rsquo;s joyous response when lo and behold, the Australian PM wrote back assuring him the government was on to it. The PM&rsquo;s office has a different story. &lsquo;No relationship&rsquo; The political snaps were not evidence of any friendship or collaboration with the PM or his cabinet ministers, a spokesman for Morrison told 7NEWS. &ldquo;Mr Yuan has paid to attend events Mr Morrison attended,&rdquo; he said. As for the PM taking Yuan&rsquo;s advice, no direct correspondence had been entered into, the spokesman said. Yuan was, however, sent a letter signed by Ben Morton, the Assistant Minister to the Prime Minister, on February 10, which stated: &ldquo;The Prime Minister appreciates your offer to assist in facilitating discussions to respond to the novel Coronavirus. &ldquo;The Australian Government is already working closely with Chinese authorities and our international partners to keep Australians safe and assist China in dealing with what is a difficult and complex health crisis.&rdquo; Mysterious club 7NEWS sent a lengthy list of questions concerning this story to Yuan on Thursday morning. On Friday shortly after midday, Yuan replied that he was unable to meet 7NEWS&rsquo; deadline. &ldquo;I can only confirm that ALL of the&hellip; allegations stated&hellip;are untrue,&rdquo; he said. 7NEWS does not suggest there is any illegality involved in the import or export involved in the flights. More on 7NEWS.com.au 7NEWS is also seeking clarification on whether the &ldquo;local and Chinese business communities&rdquo; Yuan says were behind the shipment are part of consortium of entrepreneurs belonging to a mysterious club. The Australia China Entrepreneurs Club describes itself as &ldquo;a premium business platform to share privileged information with like-minded entrepreneurs and investors who are already very influential, affluent and extremely successful business owners, senior managers and executives in both Australia and China&rdquo;. With an annual individual membership of $2,500, it is open only to those who can prove they own more than $5 million in Australian assets, and prove their credentials as &ldquo;influential high-net-worth-individuals&rdquo; wielding significant business and political clout. class="css-90kosc-StyledBlockContent eehx1jj1" id="ArticleContent"><p class="css-1316j2p-StyledParagraph enqfv0t0">It was spruiked as a mission of mercy, airlifting urgent medical supplies from Wuhan to Australia as hospitals here prepared for the full onslaught of COVID-19.</p><p class="css-1316j2p-StyledParagraph enqfv0t0">But the flight that <a class="css-10nofso-StyledTextLink ekjhzdr0" data-link-type="article-inline" target="_blank" href="https://7news.com.au/lifestyle/health-wellbeing/sydney-receives-first-flight-from-wuhan-after-china-lifts-citys-lockdown-laws-c-964409">landed at Sydney airport on the night of April 8</a>, with some 70 tonnes of PPE (Personal Protection Equipment) in its cargo hold, was, in fact, part of a commercial venture in a coronavirus-spooked market.</p><p class="css-1316j2p-StyledParagraph enqfv0t0"><a class="css-10nofso-StyledTextLink ekjhzdr0" data-link-type="article-inline" target="_blank" href="https://7news.com.au/">7NEWS.com.au</a> can now reveal that the Australian government never requested or accepted what was on the plane.</p><div class="hide-print ad-no-notice css-qyun7f-StyledAdUnitWrapper e1rlhwat0"><div hidden="" class="css-kc1cf7-StyledCenter e1rlhwat3"><div class="ad-article-mrec-one css-1ijpwc1-StyledAdUnit e1rlhwat4" hidden=""></div></div></div><p class="css-1316j2p-StyledParagraph enqfv0t0"><strong>In the video above: Wuhan plane touches down in Sydney</strong></p><p class="css-1316j2p-StyledParagraph enqfv0t0">The Suparna privately chartered plane  with its crew under strict Australian Border Force instructions not to leave the airport premises - left for its return flight to Wuhan in the early hours of the following morning.</p><p class="css-1316j2p-StyledParagraph enqfv0t0">On board was 11,298 tins of Viplus baby formula and 35,000 kgs of Tasmanian Atlantic salmon.</p><p class="css-1316j2p-StyledParagraph enqfv0t0">The Australian government will not say how much the Chinese consortium was asking for the unsolicited 500 cubic metres of cargo, comprising one million face masks, protective gowns, goggles, gloves, shoe covers, antiseptic wipes and coronavirus testing kits, all purportedly European certified.</p><figure class="css-35b5m-StyledFigure e1kk8o2i2"><picture class="e1kk8o2i1 image css-l9osnz-PictureWithIntrinsicRatio-StyledArticleImage e1gxq4w20"><source data-ratio="1163:689" Airport on April 8.</span><span> Credit: <!-- -->7NEWS</span></span></figcaption></figure><p class="css-1316j2p-StyledParagraph enqfv0t0">Whatever the figure, it was too much. The deal did not go ahead.</p><p class="css-1316j2p-StyledParagraph enqfv0t0"><a class="css-10nofso-StyledTextLink ekjhzdr0" data-link-type="article-inline" target="_blank" href="https://7news.com.au/">7NEWS.com.au</a> is unable to say to what extent the asking price reflected the impact of worldwide shortages on the price the consortium paid to its supplier, or the mark-up made by the consortium when it attempted to sell the material in Australia.</p><p class="css-1316j2p-StyledParagraph enqfv0t0">A spokesman for the Department of Health confirmed to 7NEWS.com.au it received a number of approaches offering products at inflated prices at a time when PPE supplies were in short supply.</p><p class="css-1316j2p-StyledParagraph enqfv0t0">It did not deny, however, that one of those unsolicited offers involved a shipment that arrived by plane from Wuhan in April.</p><p class="css-1316j2p-StyledParagraph enqfv0t0">The Sydney Airport Corporation has confirmed to 7NEWS there was only one flight that landed in Sydney in April from Wuhan.</p><figure class="css-35b5m-StyledFigure e1kk8o2i2"><picture class="e1kk8o2i1 image         })

flight to Australia","description":"It was spruiked as a mercy flight, airlifting urgent COVID medical supplies from Wuhan  but what really happened after the plane touched down?","dateCreated":"2020-05-01T06:05:56.000Z","datePublished":"2020-05-01T06:05:56.000Z","dateModified":"2020-05-01T09:31:40.111Z","isAccessibleForFree":true,"articleBody":"It was spruiked as a mission of mercy, airlifting urgent medical supplies from Wuhan to Australia as hospitals here prepared for the full onslaught of COVID-19. But the flight that landed at Sydney airport on the night of April 8, with some 70 tonnes of PPE (Personal Protection Equipment) in its cargo hold, was, in fact, part of a commercial venture in a coronavirus-spooked market. 7NEWS.com.au can now reveal that the Australian government never requested or accepted what was on the plane. In the video above: Wuhan plane touches down in Sydney The Suparna privately chartered plane &ndash; with its crew under strict Australian Border Force instructions not to leave the airport premises - left for its return flight to Wuhan in the early hours of the following morning. On board was 11,298 tins of Viplus baby formula and 35,000 kgs of Tasmanian Atlantic salmon. The Australian government will not say how much the Chinese consortium was asking for the unsolicited 500 cubic metres of cargo, comprising one million face masks, protective gowns, goggles, gloves, shoe covers, antiseptic wipes and coronavirus testing kits, all purportedly &ldquo;European certified&rdquo;. Whatever the figure, it was too much. The deal did not go ahead. 7NEWS.com.au is unable to say to what extent the asking price reflected the impact of worldwide shortages on the price the consortium paid to its supplier, or the mark-up made by the consortium when it attempted to sell the material in Australia. A spokesman for the Department of Health confirmed to 7NEWS.com.au it received &ldquo;a number of approaches offering products at inflated prices&rdquo; at a time when PPE supplies were in short supply. It did not deny, however, that one of those unsolicited offers involved a shipment that arrived by plane from Wuhan in April. The Sydney Airport Corporation has confirmed to 7NEWS there was only one flight that landed in Sydney in April from Wuhan. It was the one hailed as a mission of mercy by a Sydney-based Chinese entrepreneur who bears a multitude of office-bearing titles in Australia and is admired as a Chinese Communist Party stalwart in his home province of Hunan in South Central China. When 7NEWS approached Yuan &ldquo;Richard&rdquo; Zuwen on Wednesday about the fate of the cargo that arrived on a privately chartered Suparna aircraft in Sydney on April 8, he declined to give details. &ldquo;Quite a number of local and Chinese business communities have been involved in the 8 April shipment, I am not in the position to comment at this stage,&rdquo; he said in a statement. 7NEWS has also been unsuccessful in ascertaining where all that baby formula ended up, with Yuan saying it was going to be &ldquo;donated to the Hubei Youth Development Foundation which will distribute the goods to key hospitals, schools, children&rsquo;s health centres, kindergartens and nursing homes&rdquo;. Baby formula supplies have been threatened in Australia in recent months, and scenes of long queues, panic buying and even verbal and physical abuse captured outside stores seeking to ration purchases of the products. And scenes of long queues, panic buying and even verbal and physical abuse have been captured outside stores seeking to ration purchases of the products. The shortage has been blamed in some quarters on Daigou traders &ndash; people living in Australia and turning a profit by buying up products in high demand and then selling them to wealthy consumers living in mainland China. Yuan is the Executive Director of the Australia China Daigou Association. Less than a month before that plane landed, Yuan founded an organisation called the Australia China Goodwill Association. On April 9, as chair of that association, he issued a media release hailing Suparna flight Y87447 as a mission of mercy and a victory in the China-Australia bilateral relationship in a time of crisis. &ldquo;We&rsquo;re motivated by the classic Aussie response of helping a mate in need,&rdquo; he wrote. &ldquo;Our community has been honoured by the opportunity to arrange this bilateral goodwill measure &ndash; coordinated to support people in need both in Australia and China.&rdquo; Diplomatic claims Yuan said in the release the shipment was coordinated through &ldquo;Australian diplomatic&rdquo; channels. A spokesman for Prime Minister Scott Morrison has told 7NEWS Yuan was given no such diplomatic access. But Yuan appears fond of spruiking his powerful connections, having posted online an extensive collection of photos featuring the Chinese businessman meeting Australian politicians online. In a series of photos, Yuan, whose business interests include an immigration and visa agency for Chinese nationals, says he &ldquo;participated in the small reception of the Australian Prime Minister and his &lsquo;top team&rsquo; and had a chat with the PM &hellip; on &lsquo;improving Sino-Australian relations&rsquo; and other aspects&rdquo;. Yuan has also been snapped on occasion with Immigration Minister David Coleman, Trade Minister Simon Birmingham, former NSW Premier Bob Carr and even former prime minister John Howard. Here he is with the now Home Affairs Minister Peter Dutton: And here he is with former Foreign Affairs Minister Julie Bishop: Yuan&rsquo;s political connections have attracted considerable media attention in his home country. That includes a full-length profile in the Chinese Communist Party-controlled publication Chinanews.com explaining how Yuan &rdquo;helped&rdquo; the Australian PM in his handling of the coronavirus. &ldquo;Yuan Zuwen has had a relationship with Scott Morrison for many years, and he (Yuan) felt it necessary to pass him some information and ideas,&rdquo; the story, translated into English reads. Yuan wrote a letter urging the PM to be more proactive and flexible in responding to the pandemic and &ldquo;keep in mind the friendly relations between Australia and China more comprehensively.&rdquo; The story captured with enthusiasm Yuan&rsquo;s joyous response when lo and behold, the Australian PM wrote back assuring him the government was on to it. The PM&rsquo;s office has a different story. &lsquo;No relationship&rsquo; The political snaps were not evidence of any friendship or collaboration with the PM or his cabinet ministers, a spokesman for Morrison told 7NEWS. &ldquo;Mr Yuan has paid to attend events Mr Morrison attended,&rdquo; he said. As for the PM taking Yuan&rsquo;s advice, no direct correspondence had been entered into, the spokesman said. Yuan was, however, sent a letter signed by Ben Morton, the Assistant Minister to the Prime Minister, on February 10, which stated: &ldquo;The Prime Minister appreciates your offer to assist in facilitating discussions to respond to the novel Coronavirus. &ldquo;The Australian Government is already working closely with Chinese authorities and our international partners to keep Australians safe and assist China in dealing with what is a difficult and complex health crisis.&rdquo; Mysterious club 7NEWS sent a lengthy list of questions concerning this story to Yuan on Thursday morning. On Friday shortly after midday, Yuan replied that he was unable to meet 7NEWS&rsquo; deadline. &ldquo;I can only confirm that ALL of the&hellip; allegations stated&hellip;are untrue,&rdquo; he said. 7NEWS does not suggest there is any illegality involved in the import or export involved in the flights. More on 7NEWS.com.au 7NEWS is also seeking clarification on whether the &ldquo;local and Chinese business communities&rdquo; Yuan says were behind the shipment are part of consortium of entrepreneurs belonging to a mysterious club. The Australia China Entrepreneurs Club describes itself as &ldquo;a premium business platform to share privileged information with like-minded entrepreneurs and investors who are already very influential, affluent and extremely successful business owners, senior managers and executives in both Australia and China&rdquo;. With an annual individual membership of $2,500, it is open only to those who can prove they own more than $5 million in Australian assets, and prove their credentials as &ldquo;influential high-net-worth-individuals&rdquo; wielding significant business and political clout.
